        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968"><meta property="og:title" content="Air-Stable High-Entropy Layered Oxide Cathode with Enhanced Cycling Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/acs.nanolett.4c00968.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="O3-type layered oxides have been extensively studied as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries due to their high reversible capacity and high initial sodium content, but they suffer from complex phase transitions and an unstable structure during sodium intercalation/deintercalation. Herein, we synthesize a high-entropy O3-type layered transition metal oxide, NaNi0.3Cu0.05Fe0.1Mn0.3Mg0.05Ti0.2O2 (NCFMMT), by simultaneously doping Cu, Mg, and Ti into its transition metal layers, which greatly increase structural entropy, thereby reducing formation energy and enhancing structural stability. The high-entropy NCFMMT cathode exhibits significantly improved cycling stability (capacity retention of 81.4% at 1C after 250 cycles and 86.8% at 5C after 500 cycles) compared to pristine NaNi0.3Fe0.4Mn0.3O2 (71% after 100 cycles at 1C), as well as remarkable air stability. Finally, the NCFMMT//hard carbon full-cell batteries deliver a high initial capacity of 103 mAh g–1 at 1C, with 83.8 mAh g–1 maintained after 300 cycles (capacity retention of 81.4%)."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/acs.nanolett.4c00968.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187f1f0e24075b-MAN"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187f1f0e24075b-MAN">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '44196cc0-120c-4798-af52-c672ab479952'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187f1f0e24075b-MAN">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span <script nonce="93187f1f0e24075b-MAN">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Air-Stable High-Entropy Layered Oxide Cathode with Enhanced Cycling Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Air-Stable High-Entropy Layered Oxide Cathode with Enhanced Cycling Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Air-Stable High-Entropy Layered Oxide Cathode with Enhanced Cycling Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Air-Stable High-Entropy Layered Oxide Cathode with Enhanced Cycling Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Air-Stable High-Entropy Layered Oxide Cathode with Enhanced Cycling Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Air-Stable High-Entropy Layered Oxide Cathode with Enhanced Cycling Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Air-Stable High-Entropy Layered Oxide Cathode with Enhanced Cycling Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Air-Stable High-Entropy Layered Oxide Cathode with Enhanced Cycling Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Air-Stable High-Entropy Layered Oxide Cathode with Enhanced Cycling Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Australia</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Shi-Xue++Dou" data-id="article_author_search">Shi-Xue Dou</a></span></div><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Chao Wu</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">Institute of Energy data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Herein, we synthesize a high-entropy O3-type layered transition metal oxide, NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCFMMT), by simultaneously doping Cu, Mg, and Ti into its transition metal layers, which greatly increase structural entropy, thereby reducing formation energy and enhancing structural stability. The high-entropy NCFMMT cathode exhibits significantly improved cycling stability (capacity retention of 81.4% at 1C after 250 cycles and 86.8% at 5C after 500 cycles) compared to pristine NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (71% after 100 cycles at 1C), as well as remarkable air stability. Finally, the NCFMMT//hard carbon full-cell batteries deliver a high initial capacity of 103 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1C, with 83.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> maintained after 300 cycles (capacity retention of 81.4%).</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D9793%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJiajia%2BZhan%252C%2BJiawen%2BHuang%252C%2BZhen%2BLi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D24%26issueNum%3D32%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.4c00968%26title%3DAir-Stable%2BHigh-Entropy%2BLayered%2BOxide%2BCathode%2Bwith%2BEnhanced%2BCycling%2BStability%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D9800%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290776&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Phase transitions"> Phase transitions </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium"> Sodium </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291675&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Stability"> Stability </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292316&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transition metals"> Transition metals </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode%20materials">cathode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Entropy&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20entropy">high entropy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Layered+Transition+Metal+Oxides&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_layered%20transition%20metal%20oxides">layered transition metal oxides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cycling+Stability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cycling%20stability">cycling stability</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><p class="first">As sustainable solutions to the escalating global energy demands, renewable energy sources are usually constrained by weather or geographic conditions, resulting in their intrinsic intermittency and variability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Therefore, advanced energy storage systems have attracted great interest in recent years, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> particularly rechargeable alkali-ion batteries, due to their high energy density and portability. For instance, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely deployed in portable electronics and electric vehicles, and their global production capacity has been expanded more than 30 times in the past decade. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a> Unfortunately, despite the high energy density <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> and remarkable cycle life achieved by state-of-the-art LIBs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> the over-reliance on LIBs raises concerns about the uneven distribution of lithium reserves and high cost of raw materials. In this case, sodium-ion batteries <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> (SIBs) are considered as promising and cost-effective alternatives to LIBs, owing to the abundant sodium reservoirs in the natural world and the similar working mechanism shared with LIBs. Toward the commercialization of SIBs, unremitting efforts have been made in developing electrode materials to accommodate the larger Na<sup>+</sup> ions (1.02 Å vs 0.76 Å for Li<sup>+</sup>), by designing structurally stable cathode materials <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> with high theoretical capacity and high voltage.</p><p>Currently, the common cathode materials in SIBs are classified into four categories: layered transition metal oxides <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> (LMOs), polyanionic compounds, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> Prussian blue and its analogs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> and organic compounds. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> Among them, layered transition metal oxides, Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TmO<sub>2</sub>, where <i>x</i> ≤ 1 and Tm represents one or multiple transition metal elements, are considered one of the most attractive candidates for SIBs due to their diverse composition, facile preparation, and high theoretical capacity. The layered structure of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TmO<sub>2</sub> consists of alternating stacking of transition metal layers TmO<sub>6</sub> and alkali metal layers NaO<sub>6</sub>. According to the difference in Na coordination environments and minimum number of transition metal layers within each unit cell, Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TmO<sub>2</sub> materials are mainly divided into O3 and P2 types, in which the O3-type LMOs have higher sodium content with higher first-cycle capacity compared to P2-type LMOs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> mitigating the sodium deficiency in the application of full-cell batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Among all kinds of O3-type LMOs, NiFeMn-based LMOs are the most widely studied due to their high specific capacity, low cost, and availability of raw materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Nevertheless, O3-type layered transition metal oxides suffer from a multistage phase transition during discharging and charging, viz., Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and deintercalation, leading to inferior structure stability and rapid capacity decay. Meanwhile, they usually show poor air stability, becoming structurally unstable upon exposure to humid air. Many efforts have been made to resolve their fast capacity fade and air sensitivity, among which element doping strategies are the most widely adopted. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> For instance, Sun <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> et al. reported Na<sub>0.912</sub>Ca<sub>0.048</sub>Ni<sub>0.320</sub>Fe<sub>0.333</sub>Mn<sub>0.330</sub>O<sub>2</sub> doped with Ca<sup>2+</sup> in the sodium layer, for which the capacity retention increased to 91.8% after 200 cycles at a current density of 130 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, compared to 67% in pristine material, attributed to the stable crystal structure framework constructed by the inactive Ca<sup>2+</sup> in sodium layers. Yang-Kook Sun et al. introduced Li<sup>+</sup> in the transition metal layers of Na[Li<sub>0.05</sub>(Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>0.95</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> which effectively facilitated sodium ion diffusion kinetics. Recently, high-entropy doping <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> emerged as another promising approach to enhance the cycling stability of O3-type LMOs, benefiting from the synergistic effects of multiple elements. By definition, a high-entropy oxide <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 20 to 23">(20−23)</a> is a single-structure solid solution composed of at least five different metallic oxides, where multiple metal cations occupy the same lattice. As the increasing number of elements generally increases the overall entropy of a substance, such a multielement mixed solid solution should become more stable, according to the Gibbs free energy equation Δ<i>G</i> = Δ<i>H</i> – <i>T</i>Δ<i>S</i>, where the increasing entropy leads to decreasing Gibbs free energy of a substance, at a constant temperature and enthalpy of formation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> For example, Yong-Sheng Hu et al. successfully prepared a high-entropy O3-type oxide, NaNi<sub>0.12</sub>Cu<sub>0.12</sub>Mg<sub>0.12</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.1</sub>Sb<sub>0.04</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> exhibiting exceptional cycling stability by maintaining 86% of its initial capacity after 200 cycles at a current density of 0.5C. Shi-Xue Dou et al. developed air-stable high-entropy Na[Li<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> delivering a remarkable reversible specific capacity of 172 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 0.1C, and its full cell with a hard carbon anode showed a capacity retention of approximately 60% after 100 cycles at 0.5C.</p><p>In this work, we report a high-entropy configuration strategy to enhance the electrochemical performance and cycling stability of an O3-type NiFeMn-based layered transition metal oxide, NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM), by simultaneously doping Cu<sup>2+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, and Ti<sup>4+</sup> into its transition metal layers to increase its structural entropy and stabilize its crystal structure. A series of oxides were prepared and studied, including Ti-doped NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Cu–Ti co-doped NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and Cu–Mg–Ti high-entropy NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCFMMT). A unique cocktail effect was observed in Cu–Mg–Ti-doped NCFMMT because of high-entropy doping, with Mg improving its structural reversibility during cycling and suppressing multistage phase transition, Ti reinforcing its crystal structure, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> and Cu enhancing its air stability. Thereby, high-entropy NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> achieved a highly reversible O3–P3 phase transition during sodium intercalation and deintercalation, with restrained cell volume change and crystal structure deterioration. As a result, the O3-type NCFMMT cathode delivered a high reversible capacity of 99 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1C, with capacity retention of 81.4% after 250 cycles or 86.8% after 500 cycles at 5C. The full-cell battery with NCFMMT cathode and a hard carbon anode showed remarkable cycling stability, retaining 93.5% of its initial capacity after 90 cycles at 1C or 81.4% after 300 cycles. Moreover, NCFMMT exhibited high air stability due to the merits of Cu with high redox potential, maintaining its structure even in exposure to air for 7 days or immersion in water for 1 h.</p><p>The pristine NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Ti-doped NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Cu,Ti co-substituted NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and Cu,Ti,Mg co-doped NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, which are denoted by NFM, NFMT, NCFMT, and NCFMMT, respectively, were all prepared by high-temperature solid-state synthesis. First, their microstructure and morphology were examined by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a–d, agglomerated particles were observed to be slightly larger in doped samples than in the pristine NFM sample. After multielement doping, the coarsening of the particle size ascribed to the entropy increase of the sample during the calcination process reduced their surface free energy, thereby promoting grain growth. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Further characterization of the NCFMMT sample was conducted through high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM), where the lattice spacing was determined to be 0.256 nm, corresponding to the 101 crystal facet of the O3 phase (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f shows its selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern, in which a periodic arrangement of diffraction spots was observed, indicating the excellent crystallinity of the synthesized NCFMMT sample. The elemental mapping in the NCFMMT sample was investigated using energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>h–m, where Ni, Fe, Mn, Cu, Mg, and Ti elements were uniformly distributed in the material, demonstrating the successful elemental doping into its lattice. To further validate the ratio of doping elements, the elemental compositions of NFM and NCFMMT samples were analyzed by ICP-OES/MS, resulting in the actual stoichiometric compositions of Na<sub>0.9</sub>Ni<sub>0.31</sub>Fe<sub>0.39</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> for NFM and Na<sub>0.91</sub>Ni<sub>0.32</sub>Fe<sub>0.11</sub>Mn<sub>0.29</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Ti<sub>0.18</sub>O<sub>2</sub> for NCFMMT, which closely aligned with the experimental expectations (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/suppl_file/nl4c00968_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S6</a>).</p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/medium/nl4c00968_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/large/nl4c00968_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. SEM images of (a) NFM, (b) NFMT, (c) NCFMT, and (d) NCFMMT. High-resolution TEM images of (e) NCFMMT and the corresponding SAED (inset). (f) The SAED pattern, (g) TEM image, and (h–m) EDS elemental mapping of NCFMMT.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/large/nl4c00968_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>To verify the crystal structure of the synthesized materials, X-ray diffraction (XRD) was performed on all samples, followed by Rietveld refinement using GSAS2 software, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a–d. The diffraction peaks of all four samples corresponded well to the O3 phase, belonging to the rhombohedral crystal system, space group <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i>. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/suppl_file/nl4c00968_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S1–S5</a> present detailed refinement results collected by Rietveld refinement, which showed Ni, Cu, Mg, Fe, Mn, and Ti elements occupying the transition-metal positions. Generally, in cathode material oxides, the more diverse the elements in the transition metal layers, the greater the structural entropy, leading to a decrease in system energy and hence stabilizing the structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e). In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f, with an increase in dopant elements in the transition metal layers, the sodium–oxygen (Na–O) bond lengths in the samples decreased, resulting in a decrease in the sodium layer spacing (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h illustrates the structural models refined for the NFM and NCFMMT samples, in which the sodium layer spacing notably decreases after co-doping of Cu, Mg, and Ti, compared to that in the pristine NFM sample.</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/medium/nl4c00968_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/large/nl4c00968_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Powder XRD patterns and Rietveld refinement plots for (a) NFM, (b) NFMT, (c) NCFMT, and (d) NCFMMT. (e) Calculated configurational entropy in a layered cathode oxide as a function of the mole fraction of component <i>n</i> in the TM site (other components in TM site are equally proportional). (f) Variation of bond length of doped samples. (g) Variation of sodium layer spacing of doped samples. (h) Structural changes of NFM after the Cu, Mg, Ti co-doping.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/large/nl4c00968_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>To evaluate the electrochemical performance of prepared samples, half-cells were assembled with sodium metal and different cathode materials and tested in a voltage range of 2–4.2 V. In terms of rate capability, the doped samples exhibited improved rate performance and cycling stability compared to  the pristine material (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a). Although pristine NFM showed an initial specific capacity of 137 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1C (where 1C = 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), a discharging capacity of merely 27 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was maintained at 10C. Moreover, after repeated cycles of the stepwise rate performance test, the discharging specific capacity of pristine NFM dropped to nearly zero, indicating the inferior rate capability of NFM due to its irreversible structural damage at large current density. In contrast, both doped NFMT and NCFMT exhibited enhanced rate capacity, while NCFMMT had the best performance among all samples, which delivered an initial discharging specific capacity of 129 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current density of 0.1C (where 1C = 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), with a reversible discharging capacity of 58 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). Impressively, NCFMMT restored its initial reversible capacity after the second cycle of the rate test. The cycling performance of all samples is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>c,d, indicating the significantly improved cycling stability because of elemental doping, particularly that of the high-entropy NCFMMT sample. At a current density of 1C, the initial specific capacity of NFM was 93 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, rapidly attenuating to 67 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles with a capacity retention of 71%. Meanwhile, the NCFMMT sample showed an initial specific capacity of 99 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and maintained 96 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles, with a capacity retention of 97% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c). The capacity decay was noticeably suppressed in the high-entropy NCFMMT sample, maintaining 81.4% of its initial capacity after 250 cycles at a current density of 1C, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/suppl_file/nl4c00968_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. At a current density of 5C, the reversible specific capacities of NFM and NCFMMT were 66 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 88 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, with capacity retention rates of 45.8% and 86.8% after 500 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d). Such a remarkable enhancement in the cycling performance of NCFMMT was attributed to high-entropy doping, which induced highly disordered distribution of transition-metal elements within the transition-metal layers in NCFMMT, meanwhile restraining the ordering of Na<sup>+</sup> vacancies and electric charges, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> thus effectively suppressing interlayer sliding and phase transition, consequently enhancing the rate capability and cycling performance of the high-entropy-doped sample.</p><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/medium/nl4c00968_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/large/nl4c00968_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Rate capability of NFM, NFMT, NCFMT, and NCFMMT. (b) Capacity–voltage curves of NCFMMT samples at different current densities. Cycling performance at (c) 1C and (d) 5C rate of NFM, NFMT, NCFMT, and NCFMMT. Charge–discharge curves of the first five cycles of (e) NFM, (f) NFMT, (g) NCFMT, and (h) NCFMMT cathodes at 0.1C during cycling between 2.0 and 4.2 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/large/nl4c00968_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The charge–discharge curves of NFM, NFMT, NCFMT, and NCFMMT are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e–h, at a current density of 0.1C and voltage range of 2–4.2 V. It is obvious that a voltage plateau presents at 4.1 V in the charge–discharge curves of both pristine NFM and Ti-doped NFMT samples, indicating that the phase transition occurred in these materials at 4.1 V. In comparison, such a voltage plateau disappeared in the charge–discharge curves of Cu,Ti co-doped NCFMT and Cu,Mg,Ti co-doped NCFMMT samples, which implied the inhibition of the multiphase transition. Noteworthily, a continuously decaying voltage plateau at 4.1 V was clearly observed in NFM, which corresponds to the irreversible phase transition from P3 to OP2. Meanwhile, a narrowed voltage plateau region at low voltage was observed in both NFMT and NCFMT, which was attributed to the suppression of the two-phase reaction by titanium element doping. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> The voltage plateau of NCFMMT disappeared at high voltage, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> indicating that a stable solid solution was formed at high voltage as a result of multielement doping, mitigating irreversible two-phase reaction and improving cycle stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> Furthermore, among these materials, NCFMMT possessed a highly overlapping pattern of the voltage–capacity curves after multiple charge–discharge cycles, demonstrating the superior structure stability and suppressed capacity fading because of multielement doping.</p><p>To investigate the impact of doping on the enhanced cycling stability of NCFMMT, the structural changes and phase transitions of NCFMMT and NFM were monitored during the sodium deintercalation–intercalation process, using ex situ X-ray diffraction within 2.0–4.2 V in the first charge–discharge cycle, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>. Initially, during the charging of pristine NFM, the 003 and 006 characteristic peaks of the O3 phase gradually shifted to lower angles, indicating expanded interlayer spacing due to enhanced electrostatic repulsion between negatively charged oxygen layers after sodium ion extraction. Concurrently, the 104 and 012 peaks shifted to higher angles, indicating interplanar distance contraction due to transition metal oxidation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> This two-phase transition from the O3 to P3 structure, observed in the first voltage plateau region (2.8–3.1 V) of the charge–discharge profile, resulted in a decrease in intensity of characteristic peaks of the pristine O3 phase, while the P3-phase (003) emerged. Upon reaching 3.4 V, all O3-phase characteristic peaks disappeared, indicating completion of the O3–P3 phase transformation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> When charged to 4.1 V, the (003)P3 peak continued splitting, signifying structure evolution from P3 to OP2. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> In contrast, NCFMMT underwent only phase transformation of the O3–P3 phase during the first cycle, with two voltage plateaus observed within the 2–4.2 V range. Comparison of XRD contour maps during the first cycle showed that NCFMMT experienced smaller changes in sodium interlayer spacing compared to NFM, evidenced by the smaller variation in 2θ value when (003)O3 evolved into (003)P3 (0.761° for NFM vs 0.615° for NCFMMT). Thus, the improved cycling stability of NCFMMT primarily stemmed from its simpler phase transformation and robust crystal structure during sodium intercalation/deintercalation after multielement doping.</p><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/medium/nl4c00968_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/large/nl4c00968_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Ex situ XRD patterns of (a) NFM and (c) NCFMMT upon Na<sup>+</sup> desertion/insertion between 2.0 and 4.2 V. Contour maps of main characteristic peaks of (b) NFM and (d) NCFMMT.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/large/nl4c00968_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>Moreover, the structure stability of NCFMMT was investigated by density functional theory (DFT) calculation by comparing the formation energy of NFM and NCFMMT. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="Equations 1">Equations 1</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a> represent the chemical equations for the synthesis of NFM and NCFMMT during calcination, respectively, with their formation energy per formula unit (f.u.) denoted by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eqs 3">eqs 3</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="4">4</a>, respectively,</p><div mathvariant="normal">form</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mn>2</mn></msub></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div>where <i>E</i><sub>NFM</sub>, <i>E</i><sub>NCFMMT</sub>, <i>E</i><sub>Na<sub>2</sub>O</sub>, <i>E</i><sub>NiO</sub>, <i>E</i><sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></sub>, <i>E</i><sub>MnO<sub>2</sub></sub>, <i>E</i><sub>CuO</sub>, <i>E</i><sub>MgO,</sub> and <i>E</i><sub>TiO<sub>2</sub></sub> are the DFT total energies per f.u. of NFM, NCFMMT, Na<sub>2</sub>O, NiO, Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, MnO<sub>2</sub>, CuO, MgO, and TiO<sub>2</sub>, respectively. According to DFT calculations, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/suppl_file/nl4c00968_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> shows that the formation energy of NCFMMT was more negative than that of NFM, with a difference of −1.825 eV/f.u., which indicated that the NCFMMT sample is thermodynamically more stable. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, in the voltammetry profiles of the first three cycles, obtained at a scan rate of 0.1 mV/s within voltage range of 2–4.2 V, two pairs of redox peaks were observed at 2.8/3.0 V and 3.9/4.1 V in NFM, which were attributed to Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> respectively. The peak current of the oxidation peak at high voltage continued to decrease, which corresponded to the continuous migration of iron ions in the transition metal layer, accompanied by the irreversible phase transition of P3-OP2. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> On the other hand, the CV patterns of high-entropy NCFMMT were highly overlapped, with only a pair of redox peaks sighted. This indicated the stabilized crystal structure after high-entropy substitution, and the migration of iron ions in the metal layer was prevented, suppressing the undesirable phase transition and improving the cycle reversibility.<p></p><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/medium/nl4c00968_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/large/nl4c00968_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV curves of NFM and NCFMMT at 0.1 mV/s scan rate. CV curves of (b) NFM and (c) NCFMMT at various scan rates. (d) Relationship of peak current and square root scan rate. (e) <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> of NFM and NCFMMT. (f) GITT results for the first cycle and the calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of NFM and NCFMMT. (g) Schematic diagram of a Na-ion full cell. (h) Cycling performance of NCFMMT/HC full cell. (i) Comparison of full-cell performance of reported cathode materials.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/asset/images/large/nl4c00968_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>To reveal the improved electrode kinetics of the NCFMMT sample after high-entropy doping, both cyclic voltammetry (CV) at various scan rates and the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) were employed for the NFM and NCFMMT samples. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b,c, the CV curves of NFM and NCFMMT samples at different scan rates (0.1 mV/s, 0.2 mV/s, 0.3 mV/s, 0.4 mV/s, 0.5 mV/s, 0.6 mV/s, and 0.7 mV/s) demonstrated a strong linear correlation of the anodic and cathodic peak current with the square root of the scan rate, indicating diffusion-controlled behavior of Na intercalation/deintercalation in NFM and NCFMMT (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d). According to the Randles–Sevcik equation, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> the apparent Na diffusion coefficient was calculated, which yielded 1.96 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and 2.8 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> for NFM and NCFMMT, respectively, during the Na deintercalation process and 0.77 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and 1.09 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> for NFM and NCFMMT, respectively, during the Na intercalation process. Obviously, the high-entropy doped NCFMMT sample possessed a higher average apparent Na diffusion coefficient, as visualized in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e, proving its boosted Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation kinetics. Subsequently, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests were conducted on both samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f). During the charging process, the Na diffusion coefficient of the NFM sample steadily increased with increasing voltage until 4 V, followed by a sharp drop, which corresponded to the P3–OP2 phase transition, indicating that the appearance of the OP2 phase is unfavorable for sodium ion diffusion. In the NCFMMT sample, a sudden dip of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient occurred near 3 V, corresponding to the O3–P3 phase transition, which was consistent with the results from ex situ XRD. Even though the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of NCFMMT substantially lowered during the O3–P3 phase transition, the fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in its P3 phase still ensured a high average Na diffusion coefficient of NCFMMT in the charging process. The average Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients calculated by GITT were 6.07 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and 4.2 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> for NCFMMT and NFM, respectively, during discharging (intercalation) and 7.47 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and 2.48 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> for NCFMMT and NFM, respectively, during charging (deintercalation). Therefore, both CV and GITT tests revealed improved electrode kinetics in the high-entropy doped NCFMMT sample.</p><p>To validate the practical application of high-entropy doped NCFMMT, full-cell batteries were fabricated using the NCFMMT cathode and a hard carbon anode, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g, for electrochemical evaluation. Noteworthily, the hard carbon anode was presodiated before full-cell assembly to compensate the sodium consumption for SEI formation during the initial cycles. The hard carbon anode exhibited decent cycling stability in the half cells with sodium metal, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/suppl_file/nl4c00968_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/suppl_file/nl4c00968_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, as well as a high reversible capacity of 290 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> within the voltage range of 0.01–2 V, with an initial Coulombic efficiency of 70%. The full-cell batteries with the high-entropy NCFMMT cathode and the hard carbon anode <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 40 to 46">(40−46)</a> delivered a high initial capacity of 103 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/suppl_file/nl4c00968_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), with 96 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> initial capacity maintained after 90 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/suppl_file/nl4c00968_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) and 83.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> retained after 300 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h), corresponding to capacity retention of 93.5% and 81.4%, respectively. Furthermore, the air stability of the synthesized samples, including NFM, NFMT, NCFMT, and NCFMMT, were investigated. Commonly, sodium ions within the O3-type cathodes are susceptible to diffusing to the surface in humid air and then reacting with CO<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O, and other substances to form Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>/NaHCO<sub>3</sub>, leading to structural changes of the materials and deteriorated electrochemical performance. In this study, the four samples were intentionally exposed to air for several days or immersed in water for an hour and then characterized by XRD. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/suppl_file/nl4c00968_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>, the pristine NFM sample exhibited air instability with a change of crystal structure and precipitation of NaHCO<sub>3</sub> substances after its exposure to air or immersion in water. In contrast, NCFMT and NCFMMT samples demonstrated excellent stability in air, attributed to the elevated oxidation–reduction potential and enhanced antioxidation activity from doping of Cu<sup>2+</sup>, which suppressed the chemical extraction of sodium ions from the lattice. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a></p><p class="last">In summary, an O3-type layered transition metal oxide, NCFMMT, was successfully prepared by a high-entropy doping strategy and utilized as a cathode material for sodium-ion batteries. By simultaneously doping copper, magnesium, and titanium into the transition metal layer of the pristine NFM, the synthesized high-entropy NCFMMT cathode exhibited significantly improved cycling performance and air stability (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00968/suppl_file/nl4c00968_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S7</a>). The O3-type NCFMMT cathode demonstrated a superior reversible capacity of 99 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 1C, with an exceptional capacity retention of 97% after 100 cycles, or that of 86.8% after 500 cycles at a high current density of 5C. The full-cell battery assembled with the NCFMMT cathode and hard carbon anode exhibited excellent cycling stability, with 81.4% retention of its initial capacity after 300 cycles at 1C, as well as high air stability. The comprehensive enhancement in the electrochemical performance of NCFMMT cathode was synergistically achieved by multiple elements doped into its transition metal layers, among which the copper and titanium doping reinforced its air stability and structure stability, respectively, while the magnesium doping improved its cycling reversibility and suppressed the multistage phase transitions during the sodium intercalation/deintercalation process. Thereby, this research proves the viability of the high-entropy doping strategy in elevating the electrochemical performance and stability of a layered transition metal oxide cathode by selecting appropriate metal elements and provides novel insight for developing stable and high-energy sodium-ion batteries.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div                 10.1016/0022-4596(85)90194-X</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DMendiboure%252C%2BA.%253B%2BDelmas%252C%2BC.%253B%2BHagenmuller%252C%2BP.%2BJ.%2BSolid%2BState%2BChem.%2B1985%252C%2B57%252C%2B323%25E2%2580%2593331%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.4c00968&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2F0022-4596%2885%2990194-X&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical intercalation and deintercalation of NaxMnO2 bronzes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mendiboure, A.; Delmas, C.; Hagenmuller, P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Solid State Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1985</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">57</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">323-31</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JSSCBI</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0022-4596</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Various NaxMnO2 bronzes were electrochem. deintercalated.  Na0.40MnO2 has a channel structure which is maintained for a large intercalation range (0.30 ≤ x ≤ 0.58).  To explain the upper intercalation limit, an ordered Na distribution between 2 types of Na+ sites is proposed.  Na0.70MnO2 and α-NaMnO2 have lamellar structures of P2 and O'3 types.  During intercalation, the original P2 type is maintained for 0.45 ≤ x ≤ 0.85, while 2 reversible structural transitions are obsd. for α-NaMnO2.  A similar behavior occurs during the deintercalation of the high-temp. β-NaMnO2 variety.  In each case of the structural transition, the double octahedral layers remain unchanged.  Electronic localization (increased by Mn3+ Jahn-Teller effect) tends to trap the Na+ and therefore increases the relaxation time of the investigated materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202301975"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J. Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y. K.</span></span> <i>Adv.
